<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Baseball park</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marlins_First_Pitch_at_Marlins_Park,_April_4,_2012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1360" data-file-width="2048" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Marlins_First_Pitch_at_Marlins_Park%2C_April_4%2C_2012.jpg/300px-Marlins_First_Pitch_at_Marlins_Park%2C_April_4%2C_2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Marlins_First_Pitch_at_Marlins_Park%2C_April_4%2C_2012.jpg/450px-Marlins_First_Pitch_at_Marlins_Park%2C_April_4%2C_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Marlins_First_Pitch_at_Marlins_Park%2C_April_4%2C_2012.jpg/600px-Marlins_First_Pitch_at_Marlins_Park%2C_April_4%2C_2012.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marlins_First_Pitch_at_Marlins_Park,_April_4,_2012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Marlins_Park" title="Marlins Park">Marlins Park</a>, the second newest ballpark in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a>, behind only <a href="/wiki/SunTrust_Park" title="SunTrust Park">SunTrust Park</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <b>baseball park</b>, also known as a <b>ballpark</b> or <b>diamond</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Stadium" title="Stadium">venue</a> where <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a> is played. A baseball park consists of the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_field" title="Baseball field">playing field</a> and the surrounding spectator seating. While the diamond and the areas denoted by white painted lines adhere to strict rules, guidelines for the rest of the field are flexible.</p>
<p>The term "ballpark" sometimes refers either to the entire structure, or sometimes to just the playing field. A <a href="/wiki/Home_run" title="Home run">home run</a> where the player makes it around the bases, and back to home plate, without the ball leaving the playing field is typically called an "inside-the-park" home run. Sometimes a home run over the fence is called "out of the ballpark", but that phrase more often means a home run that clears the stands and lands outside the building. The playing field is most often called the "ballfield", though the term is often used interchangeably with "ballpark" when referring to a small local or youth league facility.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_characteristics">General characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: General characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_playing_field">The playing field</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The playing field">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_diamond.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="650" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Baseball_diamond.svg/350px-Baseball_diamond.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Baseball_diamond.svg/525px-Baseball_diamond.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Baseball_diamond.svg/700px-Baseball_diamond.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_diamond.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram of a baseball infield</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baseball_field" title="Baseball field">Baseball field</a></div>
<p>A baseball field can be referred to as a diamond. The infield is a rigidly structured <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamond_(geometry)" title="Diamond (geometry)">diamond</a> of dirt containing the three bases, home plate, and the pitchers mound. The space between the bases and home is normally a grass surface, save for the dirt mound in the center. Some ballparks, like Toronto's <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Centre" title="Rogers Centre">Rogers Centre</a>, have grass or artificial turf between the bases, and dirt only around the bases and pitcher's mound. Others, such as <a href="/wiki/Koshien_Stadium" title="Koshien Stadium">Koshien Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hy%C5%8Dgo_Prefecture" title="Hyōgo Prefecture">Hyōgo Prefecture</a>, Japan, have an entirely dirt infield.</p>
<p>Two white lines run out from the home plate area, aligned with first and third bases. These are the <i>foul lines</i> or <i>base lines</i>, usually differentiated by referring to them as the first base line, or the third base line. If a ball hit by the batter lands outside of the space between these two lines, or rolls out of this space before reaching first or third base, the ball is "foul" (meaning it is dead and the play is over). If it lands between or on the lines, it is "fair". At the end of the lines are two foul poles, which help the umpires judge whether a ball is fair or foul. These "foul poles" are actually in fair territory, so a ball that hits them on the fly is a home run (if hit on the bounce, it is instead an automatic double).</p>
<p>On either side of home plate are the two batter's boxes (left-handed and right-handed.) This is where the batter stands when at bat. Behind home is the catcher's box, where the catcher and the home plate umpire stand.</p>
<p>Next to first and third base are two coaches' boxes, where the first and third base coaches guide the baserunners, generally with gestures or shouts. As the baserunner faces away from the outfield when running from second base to third, they cannot see where the ball is, and must look to the third base coach on whether to run, stop, or slide.</p>
<p>Farther from the infield on either side are the dugouts, where the teams and coaches sit when they're not on the field. They are named such because, at the professional levels, this seating is below the level of the playing field so as to not block the view from prime spectator seating locations. In amateur parks, the dugouts may be above-ground wooden or <a href="/wiki/Concrete_masonry_unit" title="Concrete masonry unit">CMU</a> structures with seating inside, or simply benches behind a chain link fence.....No</p>
<p>Beyond the infield and between the foul lines is a large grass outfield, generally twice the depth of the infield. The playing field is bordered by fences of varying height. The infield fences are in foul territory, and a ball hit over them isn't a home run; consequently, they are often lower than the outfield fences to provide a better view for spectators. Sometimes, the outfield fence is made higher in certain areas to compensate for a close proximity to the batter.</p>
<p>In professional parks, the field is surrounded by an area roughly 10 feet (3.0 m) wide made of dirt or rubberized track surface called a "<a href="/wiki/Warning_track" title="Warning track">warning track</a>". Originally used in <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium_(1923)" title="Yankee Stadium (1923)">Yankee Stadium</a> in 1923 as an actual footrace track,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> it is now present in all major league ballparks. This change in terrain warns a fielder, who is watching a ball in the air, that the wall is near, avoiding possible injury.</p>
<p>Beyond the outfield fence in professional parks is an area called the <a href="/wiki/Batter%27s_eye" title="Batter's eye">batter's eye</a>. To ensure the batter can see the white ball, the batter's eye contains no seating, and is a darker color. This can be anything from a dark wall to a grassy slope.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seating">Seating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Seating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Today, in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a>, a multi-tiered seating area, a <a href="/wiki/Grandstand" title="Grandstand">grandstand</a>, surrounds the infield. How far this seating extends down the baselines or around the foul poles varies from park to park. In minor league parks, the grandstands are notably smaller, proportional to expected sizes of crowds compared with the major leagues.</p>
<p>The seating beyond the outfield fence generally differs from the grandstand, though some multi-purpose or jewel box parks have the grandstand surround the entire field. This area could contain inexpensive bleacher seats, smaller grandstands, or simply inclined seating. In local ballparks, there are often simply a set or two of aluminum bleachers on the first-base and third-base sides.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variations">Variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Distinctive from "goal games" such as <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">football</a> and <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">soccer</a>, which have fixed-size playing areas, the infield is the only rigidly laid-out part of the field. Like its English relative, <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a>, there is significant flexibility in the shape and size of the rest of the playing area.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yankee_Stadium_(29_September_2007).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Yankee_Stadium_%2829_September_2007%29.jpg/225px-Yankee_Stadium_%2829_September_2007%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Yankee_Stadium_%2829_September_2007%29.jpg/338px-Yankee_Stadium_%2829_September_2007%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Yankee_Stadium_%2829_September_2007%29.jpg/450px-Yankee_Stadium_%2829_September_2007%29.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yankee_Stadium_(29_September_2007).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Picture of old <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium_(1923)" title="Yankee Stadium (1923)">Yankee Stadium</a> showing its left field fence, which was famous for being farther than the right.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To prevent "cheap" home runs, baseball leagues may specify a minimum distance from home plate to the outfield fences. Generally, the higher the skill level, the deeper the minimum dimensions must be, to prevent an excess of home runs. In the major leagues, a rule was passed in 1958<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> that compelled any new fields built after that point to have a minimum distance of 325 feet (99 m) from home plate to the fences in left and right field, and 400 feet (120 m) to center. (Rule 1.04, Note(a)). This rule was passed to avoid situations like the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Memorial_Coliseum#Other_notable_uses" title="Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum">Los Angeles Coliseum</a>, which was 251 ft (77 m). down the left field line.</p>
<p>However, with the opening of Baltimore's <a href="/wiki/Oriole_Park_at_Camden_Yards" title="Oriole Park at Camden Yards">Camden Yards</a> (1992), the "minimum distance" rule began to be ignored.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> One factor may be<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> that the quaint, "retro" look of Camden Yards, with its irregular measurements, proved to be very popular, along with a traditionalist backlash against the symmetrical, multi-purpose, "cookie-cutter" stadiums. Since the opening of Camden Yards, many other "retro" stadiums have been built, each with asymmetrical fences. These distances vary from park to park, and can even change drastically in the same park. One of the most famous examples is the original Yankee Stadium, whose odd-shaped plot of land caused right field to be over 100 feet (30 m) shorter than left, although this difference lessened over time. The rectangular <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds</a> had extremely short distance down the lines, 258 ft (79 m). to right and 280 ft (85 m). to left. In contrast, the deepest part of center field was nearly 500 ft (150 m). from home plate.</p>
<p>Older ballparks, such as <a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a>, were grandfathered in and allowed to keep their original dimensions. Also, new parks have sometimes received special dispensation to deviate from these rules. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium" title="Yankee Stadium">second Yankee Stadium</a>, built 2009, used the same dimensions as <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium_(1923)" title="Yankee Stadium (1923)">the original Yankee Stadium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The heights of the fences can also vary greatly, the most famous example being the 37-foot (11 m)-high <a href="/wiki/Green_Monster" title="Green Monster">Green Monster</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a>'s left field. Such tall fences are often used to stop easy home runs in a section of the ballpark where the distances from home are shorter, or where there is little space between the field and the street beyond. Some in-play scoreboards and high fences reached 50 to 60 feet (18 m), whereas a few outfields were even lined with hedges rather than normal fences or walls. The <a href="/wiki/Hubert_H._Humphrey_Metrodome" title="Hubert H. Humphrey Metrodome">Hubert H. Humphrey Metrodome</a>, when set up for baseball, had a 23-foot (7.0 m) right field "fence" that was actually a relatively thin blue plastic sheet covering folded up football seats. It was often called the "Baggie" or "Hefty bag".</p>
<p>Some ballparks have irregularly shaped fences. Ballparks may have round swooping fences or rigidly angles fences, or possibly a significant change in direction or irregular angle. For example, the center field stands and the left field stands at <a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a> meet at an uneven angle, creating an indentation (called "the triangle") that angles sharply back into the stands. In <a href="/wiki/Citi_Field" title="Citi Field">Citi Field</a> and <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Park" title="AT&amp;T Park">AT&amp;T Park</a>, part of the right field fence juts unevenly into the outfield as if the builders were trying to create an unpredictable ricochet effect for balls hit against it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.baseballexplained.com_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.baseballexplained.com-3">[3]</a></sup> Some "retro" parks, such as <a href="/wiki/Globe_Life_Park_in_Arlington" title="Globe Life Park in Arlington">Globe Life Park in Arlington</a>, throw in a sudden and small inward turn (often referred to as a jog) just to give a little quirkiness to the design.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Milwaukee's <a href="/wiki/Miller_Park_(Milwaukee)" title="Miller Park (Milwaukee)">Miller Park</a> was designed, with the help of former player <a href="/wiki/Robin_Yount" title="Robin Yount">Robin Yount</a>, to promote <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra_base_hit" title="Extra base hit">extra base hits</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Originally (mostly in the old jewel box parks) these variations resulted from the shape of the property where the park was constructed. If there was a street beyond left field, the distance to the left field fence would be shorter, and if the distance was too short, the fence would be higher. For example, in the old <a href="/wiki/Griffith_Stadium" title="Griffith Stadium">Griffith Stadium</a> in Washington, D.C., part of center field had to be built around a cluster of apartment houses and the result was a rather large angular indentation in the left-center field fence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.baseballexplained.com_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-www.baseballexplained.com-3">[3]</a></sup> Now, these variations are mostly influenced by the specifications and whims of the designers. New "retro" parks, which try to recapture the feel of the jewel box parks, are often designed to have these quirks.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baseball1866.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="450" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Baseball1866.JPG/250px-Baseball1866.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Baseball1866.JPG/375px-Baseball1866.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Baseball1866.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baseball1866.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Elysian Fields</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Baseball was originally played in open fields or public parks. The genesis of modern baseball is conventionally connected with <a href="/wiki/Elysian_Fields,_Hoboken,_New_Jersey" title="Elysian Fields, Hoboken, New Jersey">Elysian Fields</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hoboken,_New_Jersey" title="Hoboken, New Jersey">Hoboken, New Jersey</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> a large public park where the businessmen of New York City gathered from time to time to play organized baseball games and <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a> matches, starting around the mid-1840s. The name "Field" or "Park" was typically attached to the names of the early ballparks.</p>
<p>With the beginnings of professional baseball, the ballfield became part of a complex including fixed spectator seating areas, and an enclosure to restrict access to paying customers, as with a fairgrounds. The name "Grounds" began to be attached to ballparks, starting with the <a href="/wiki/Union_Grounds" title="Union Grounds">Union Grounds</a> in 1862.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The suffixes "Field" and "Park" were still used, but many professional ballparks were "Grounds". The last surviving major league "Grounds" was the <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds</a> in New York City, which was razed in 1964.</p>
<p>The term "stadium" had been used since ancient times, typically for a running track and its seating area. As college football gained in popularity, the smaller college playing fields and/or running tracks (which also frequently had the suffix "Field") gave way to large stadiums, many of them built during the sport's "boom" of the 1920s. Major league baseball enjoyed a similar boom. One of the first major league ballparks to be called a "stadium" was actually the Polo Grounds, which was temporarily renamed Brush Stadium from its reconstruction in 1911 until the death of owner John T. Brush in the 1920s. By then, the most famous baseball "stadium" of them all had been constructed: <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium_(1923)" title="Yankee Stadium (1923)">Yankee Stadium</a>. From that point until the retro building boom of the 1990s, the suffix "Stadium" was used for almost every new major league ballpark, and was sometimes applied to the old ones, such as <a href="/wiki/Shibe_Park" title="Shibe Park">Shibe Park</a>, which was renamed Connie Mack Stadium in 1954.</p>
<p>The suffix "Dome" was also used for the indoor stadiums constructed from the 1960s onward. The official names of those arenas also often included the word "Stadium", such as the Houston <a href="/wiki/Astrodome" title="Astrodome">Astrodome</a>, whose formal name was "Harris County Domed Stadium" in 1965; the <a href="/wiki/Kingdome" title="Kingdome">Kingdome</a>, whose formal name was "King County Domed Stadium", and the Metrodome, for which the Minneapolis highway signs directed the driver to "Metrodome Stadium". The retro era of the 1990s and early 2000s saw a return to the original arboreal terms, even to domed structures such as <a href="/wiki/Safeco_Field" title="Safeco Field">Safeco Field</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miller_Park_(Milwaukee)" title="Miller Park (Milwaukee)">Miller Park</a>.</p>
<p>There is little consistency in the choice between "Field" and "Park". Organizations appear to choose based on which suffix "sounds" better for a given ballpark name.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> For example, Houston's <a href="/wiki/Minute_Maid_Park" title="Minute Maid Park">Minute Maid Park</a> was originally named "Enron Field".</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Settings">Settings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Settings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Organized baseball has been a popular spectator sport from its beginning<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. Seating area design is affected by many variables, including required capacity, audience access, and road traffic. Early ballparks like Elysian Fields were far from the city center. Each game was an event, and fans traveled by public transit to watch the game.</p>
<p>With the growth of professional leagues, and consequent growth in the quantity of games, each game became less of an event, and fan convenience became more important.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Many professional ballparks were built either near the city center, or in working-class neighborhoods, based on expected economic level of the average fan. Consequently, the classic ballparks typically had little space for automobiles, as it was expected that most fans would take mass transit to the games, a situation that still prevails at Boston's <a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a> and Chicago's <a href="/wiki/Wrigley_Field" title="Wrigley Field">Wrigley Field</a>, for example. Some early ballparks, such as Brooklyn's <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Park" title="Eastern Park">Eastern Park</a>, were abandoned because the trolley lines did not go out far enough and the team was not performing well enough for people to tolerate the inconvenience.</p>
<p>As fans became more affluent, and especially as they moved to the suburbs and bought cars, the lack of parking became an important issue. Some ballparks remedied this problem through the construction of parking garages in the vicinity, or building new ballparks with ample parking. Others built ballparks in the suburbs, typically with large parking areas. The ballpark/stadium thus became an "island" in an "ocean" of parking space.</p>
<p>The modern "retro" trend seeks to cover all the bases: an urban location, with plenty of parking <i>and</i> public transportation available.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_baseball_parks">Types of baseball parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Types of baseball parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wooden_ballparks">Wooden ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Wooden ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WorldSeries1903-640.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="428" data-file-width="648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/WorldSeries1903-640.jpg/250px-WorldSeries1903-640.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/WorldSeries1903-640.jpg/375px-WorldSeries1903-640.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/WorldSeries1903-640.jpg/500px-WorldSeries1903-640.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WorldSeries1903-640.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Huntington Avenue Grounds during the <a href="/wiki/1903_World_Series" title="1903 World Series">1903 World Series</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first professional baseball venues were large <b>wooden ballparks</b> with seats mounted on wood platforms. Although known for being constructed out of wood, they featured iron columns for better support. Some included one tier of inclined seating, topped with either a flat roof or, in some instances, a small upper tier. The outfield was bordered by tall walls or fences covered in advertisements, much like today's minor league parks. These advertisements were sometimes fronted with bleacher seats, or "bleaching boards". Wood, while prone to decomposition, was a relatively inexpensive material, as there was no official governing body for professional baseball at the time. However, the use of wood as the primary material presented a major problem, especially as baseball continued to thrive. Over time, the wooden stands aged and dried. Many parks caught fire, and some were leveled completely. This problem, along with the popularization of baseball and expectations for long-term use of the parks were major factors that drove the transition to the new standard materials for ballparks: steel and concrete.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some famous wooden parks, such as the <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds III</a> in New York and <a href="/wiki/Baker_Bowl" title="Baker Bowl">National League Park</a> in Philadelphia, burned and were rebuilt with fire-resistant materials (Polo Grounds IV and Baker Bowl). Others were simply abandoned in favor of new structures built elsewhere. These new fire-resistant parks often lasted for many decades, and (retrospectively) came to be known as "jewel boxes".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Since there are no more professional ballparks in existence left with this architectural trend, it's basically a dead design.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewel_box_ballparks">Jewel box ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Jewel box ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(July 2010)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1063" data-file-width="1600" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg/225px-Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg/338px-Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg/450px-Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fenway_from_Legend%27s_Box.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Picture of <a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a>. The famed <a href="/wiki/Green_Monster" title="Green Monster">Green Monster</a> is the left-field fence. It is the oldest active ballpark in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The ballparks built or rebuilt of concrete and steel (albeit with wooden seats) after the days of the wooden ballpark are now known as the <b>jewel box ballparks</b> or <b>classic parks</b>. These parks are said by many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2014)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> to embody the golden age of baseball. They are known for an intimate feel, and major use of exposed steel, brick, and stone. The first of these was <a href="/wiki/Shibe_Park" title="Shibe Park">Shibe Park</a>, which opened in 1909 in Philadelphia. Another Philadelphia ballpark, the <a href="/wiki/Baker_Bowl" title="Baker Bowl">Baker Bowl</a>, which opened in 1895, used steel and brick instead of wood as the primary construction materials, and is considered<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (January 2015)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> the forerunner of the jewel box parks.</p>
<p>Two-tiered grandstands became much more prevalent in this era<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2014)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup>. These decks were typically held up by steel pillars that obstructed the view from some seats in the lower level. However, because of these supports, the upper decks could come very close to the field, giving the ballpark a more intimate feel. Two tiers was the standard for decades, until the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a> built <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium_(1923)" title="Yankee Stadium (1923)">Yankee Stadium</a>. To accommodate the large crowds <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> drew, Yankee Stadium was the largest ballpark in baseball, and was built with three tiers. This became the new standard until some recently built<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2014)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> parks reverted to two.</p>
<p>Most Jewel Box Parks were built to fit the constraints of actual city blocks, resulting in asymmetrical outfield dimensions<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text in the vicinity of this tag needs examples for clarification (November 2014)">examples needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. The exceptions were Shibe Park and <a href="/wiki/Comiskey_Park" title="Comiskey Park">Comiskey Park</a>, which were built on rectangular city blocks that were large enough to accommodate left/right field symmetry.</p>
<p>Other sports, such as <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">soccer</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">football</a>, were often played at these sites (the <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds</a> and Yankee Stadium were, for example, designed to accommodate football). In contrast to the later multi-purpose parks, the seats were generally angled in a configuration suitable for baseball. The "retro" ballparks built in the 1990s and beyond are an attempt to capture the feel of the jewel box parks. The only jewel box parks still used by Major League Baseball are <a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wrigley_Field" title="Wrigley Field">Wrigley Field</a>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-purpose_ballparks">Multi-purpose ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Multi-purpose ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ThreeRiversStadium.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/ThreeRiversStadium.jpg/250px-ThreeRiversStadium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/ThreeRiversStadium.jpg/375px-ThreeRiversStadium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/ThreeRiversStadium.jpg/500px-ThreeRiversStadium.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>

<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multi-purpose_stadium" title="Multi-purpose stadium">Multi-purpose stadium</a></div>

<p>From the 1960s until the arrival of retro parks in 1992, baseball built many <b>multi-purpose ballparks</b>. Also derisively known as "concrete donuts", "cookie-cutters", or "giant ashtrays", they were usually tall and circular or square structures made entirely of, usually bare, reinforced concrete. The parks were built to hold baseball as well as football, soccer, and other sports. One of the earliest baseball stadiums that incorporated this type of design was <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Stadium" title="Cleveland Stadium">Cleveland Stadium</a> (built 1932), which featured an oval grandstand that was more friendly to goal-centered sports like football. A park built to suit all sports well, which was co-owned by the teams or the city, seemed advantageous to all, especially because it was less expensive to maintain one stadium rather than two. Some parks that were originally built for one sport were renovated to accommodate multiple sports.</p>
<p>The shape of the parks generally depended on the original use. Baseball parks that were renovated to accommodate football, like <a href="/wiki/Candlestick_Park" title="Candlestick Park">Candlestick Park</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaheim_Stadium" title="Anaheim Stadium">Anaheim Stadium</a>, were usually asymmetrically shaped. Football stadiums that were renovated to accommodate baseball, like <a href="/wiki/Hard_Rock_Stadium" title="Hard Rock Stadium">Sun Life Stadium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mile_High_Stadium" title="Mile High Stadium">Mile High Stadium</a>, were usually of a rectangular shape, though Mile High actually started its life in 1948 as a <a href="/wiki/Minor_League_Baseball" title="Minor League Baseball">Minor League Baseball</a> park known as Bears Stadium. Parks that were built to serve both were usually circular and completely enclosed on all sides. These were the parks that gained multi-purpose parks the reputation as bland cookie-cutter structures. The first of these parks was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RFK_Stadium" title="RFK Stadium">RFK Stadium</a> (named changed in January 1969 from the original DC Stadium) in the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">District of Columbia</a>. RFK is unique in that it hosted two different baseball <a href="/wiki/Team_sport" title="Team sport">teams</a>, and that it was the first to <i>originally</i> be intended for multiple sports. It is currently in use for only <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Soccer" title="Major League Soccer">Major League Soccer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/D.C._United" title="D.C. United">D.C. United</a>.</p>
<p>A notable variant among the cookie-cutter stadia was <a href="/wiki/Shea_Stadium" title="Shea Stadium">Shea Stadium</a>. One of the first parks designed and built for baseball and football, it did not feature an exterior of bare concrete, but was clad in steel. This steel was later painted blue, making Shea the only multi-purpose park to have an exterior that was not either gray or white. Also, the grandstand only extended just past the foul poles, and did not completely enclose the field. Plans were made to enclose the grandstand and build a dome, but engineers discovered that the structure could not handle the load of the proposed dome. Thus the stadium simply remained with the area behind the outfield fence open.</p>
<p>One major innovation of the multi-purpose parks was the <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilevered</a> upper deck. In earlier ballparks, the columns used to support the upper decks obstructed the view from some seats in the lower deck. The upper decks were extended upwards and the columns removed. However, even though the extension counterbalanced some of the weight, the upper decks could no longer extend as close to the field and had to be moved back. Also, the roofs could no longer be as large, and often only covered the top 15 or so rows. This exposed fans to the elements.</p>
<p>Besides the drawbacks of the cantilever design, there were other issues with these parks. With few exceptions, seating was angled to face the center of the field of play, rather than home plate. <a href="/wiki/Luxury_box" title="Luxury box">Luxury boxes</a>, which were a part of football culture, were now introduced to baseball, and were usually placed below the upper decks, pushing upper deck seating farther from the field. The furthest seats in these parks were 500 feet (150 m) or more from the plate. The capacities of these stadiums were staggeringly large, due to football's smaller schedule drawing a larger crowd per game. However, they were usually far too large for baseball, further diminishing their intimacy. Even crowds of 40,000, a fair amount for baseball, seemed sparse. Often the only times they looked full were on opening day and playoff games. Due to the rectangular shape needed for football or soccer, outfield dimensions were generally symmetrical, and even seats at field level down the lines could be far from the action.</p>
<p>Such stadiums weren't much better for football. The "cookie-cutters" with swiveling, field-level sections proved problematic. Because the front rows were too close to the field, the fans had difficulty seeing over the football benches. This was evident in the <a href="/wiki/Movable_seating" title="Movable seating">movable seating</a> sections in RFK Stadium. The first ten rows of the football configuration were practically at field level, and fans in those sections often stood up on their seats to get a better view. Other stadiums overcame this simply by covering those seats, not bothering to sell them. Despite being cost-effective, these problems eventually caused the parks to become unfashionable.</p>
<p>The multi-purpose architecture reached a climax when Toronto's SkyDome (now <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Centre" title="Rogers Centre">Rogers Centre</a>) opened in 1989. It had state-of-the-art amenities including a retractable roof, hotel, and a restaurant behind the outfield from where patrons could view the games.</p>
<p>Only one of the purely open-air multi-purpose parks is still in use by baseball today: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oakland_Coliseum" title="Oakland Coliseum">Oakland Coliseum</a>. The Athletics plan to move out of Oakland Coliseum and build a new ballpark of their own in the future.</p>
<p><i>Note: To reduce redundancy, this table does not list the indoor stadiums of the multi-purpose era in this section. However, all of the indoor ballparks of North America, which are listed in their own section, were also built as multi-purpose stadiums.</i></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_ballparks">Modern ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Modern ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dodger_Stadium_from_bullpen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3440" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Dodger_Stadium_from_bullpen.jpg/300px-Dodger_Stadium_from_bullpen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Dodger_Stadium_from_bullpen.jpg/450px-Dodger_Stadium_from_bullpen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Dodger_Stadium_from_bullpen.jpg/600px-Dodger_Stadium_from_bullpen.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dodger_Stadium_from_bullpen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dodger Stadium's varicolored seats</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While most teams turned to multi-purpose parks, some chose to build baseball-only parks. While these <b>modern ballparks</b> shirked some of the conventions of multi-purpose parks, they did include some of the new features. The most notable influences were the cantilevered upper decks, the use of seating colors other than green, and fairly plain concrete exteriors. The most important things, though, were that they had a more intimate feel to them, and they were built for baseball. While the multi-purpose parks have become all but extinct, some modern parks, such as <a href="/wiki/Dodger_Stadium" title="Dodger Stadium">Dodger Stadium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kauffman_Stadium" title="Kauffman Stadium">Kauffman Stadium</a>, have been hailed for aging beautifully. Rather than build new parks, the teams have decided instead to renovate the current structures, adding a few newer conveniences. Several of the modern parks built as such have remained in use, with no indication of being demolished.</p>
<p>While Cleveland Stadium is the ancestor to the multi-purpose ballpark, the ancestor of the modern ballpark is <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_County_Stadium" title="Milwaukee County Stadium">Milwaukee County Stadium</a>. It was the first to feature a symmetrical, round outfield fence. It also featured the rounded V-shaped grandstand and colorful seats that are common among all modern parks. Coincidentally, it also would have been one of the earlier examples of a converted park as well. It was supposed to replace a minor league facility, and serve as home of the minor league team until a major league franchise could be lured to the city. However, the Braves came to Milwaukee earlier than expected, and the minor league team never played in the stadium.</p>
<p>The first two truly modern ballparks were built by the two New York teams who moved to California, the Giants and the Dodgers. <a href="/wiki/Candlestick_Park" title="Candlestick Park">Candlestick Park</a> was created first, but was converted to a multi-purpose park to accommodate the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_49ers" title="San Francisco 49ers">49ers</a>. Dodger Stadium has been upgraded a number of times, but remains baseball-only and its original design is still largely intact.</p>
<p>Anaheim Stadium, which was initially modeled closely on Dodger Stadium, was expanded for football as was Candlestick, but once the Rams departed, most of the extra outfield seating was peeled back, returning the structure to more like its original design.</p>
<p>The original Yankee Stadium is an exceptional case. Yankee Stadium was a jewel box park, albeit a very large one. It was showing its age in the 1970s, and the stadium was extensively renovated during 1973-75, converting it into more of a modern style ballpark. Many of the characteristics that defined it as a classical jewel box were also retained, so the remodeled Stadium straddled both categories.</p>
<p>New Comiskey Park (now <a href="/wiki/Guaranteed_Rate_Field" title="Guaranteed Rate Field">Guaranteed Rate Field</a>) was the last modern ballpark to be built in North America. A series of renovations have been made to make it appear more like a retro-classic ballpark.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temporary_and_converted_ballparks">Temporary and converted ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Temporary and converted ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LA_Coliseum_1959_World_Series.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2328" data-file-width="3400" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/LA_Coliseum_1959_World_Series.jpg/250px-LA_Coliseum_1959_World_Series.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/LA_Coliseum_1959_World_Series.jpg/375px-LA_Coliseum_1959_World_Series.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/LA_Coliseum_1959_World_Series.jpg/500px-LA_Coliseum_1959_World_Series.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LA_Coliseum_1959_World_Series.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Built originally for college football and the <a href="/wiki/1932_Summer_Olympics" title="1932 Summer Olympics">Olympics</a>, the oval-shaped <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Memorial_Coliseum" title="Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum">Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum</a> became the temporary home of the relocated Dodgers in 1958.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the 1960s came the first expansion teams. While some teams expanded in cities where there were established teams with facilities that could be shared, not all were as fortunate. This led to the emergence of two distinct subsets of parks in the major leagues: <b>temporary ballparks</b> and <b>converted ballparks</b>.</p>
<p>In some cases, there are plans to build a new ballpark for the expansion team, but it will not be completed until a few years after the team is established. This may be for a few reasons, such as delays or a desire to hold off until the deal is settled. In this case, an established building is used as a temporary home, often a minor league park. The first temporary ballparks were not actually used by expansion teams but by established franchises. When the Dodgers and Giants moved to California from New York, they played in Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum and Seals Stadium respectively while Dodger Stadium and Candlestick Park were being built.</p>
<p>The other case is when, rather than building a new park, the city renovates an existing minor league or college facility, expanding it to fit a major league team. These converted ballparks are different from football stadia that were converted to facilitate baseball in that converted ballparks were originally built to be baseball only, albeit for a non-major league level. Early converted ballparks were Memorial Stadium in Baltimore, Municipal Stadium in Kansas City, and Metropolitan Stadium in Bloomington, Minnesota. All three were expanded minor league facilities, although Baltimore and the Met were constructed with the idea of expanding to major league level in mind. Kansas City was a true established minor league park that was substantially expanded to accommodate major league size crowds.</p>
<p>These two types of ballpark are distinct because of their use, not their design. Because of this, a temporary or converted ballpark can also be any of the other types: jewel box, modern, multi-purpose, etc.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indoor_ballparks">Indoor ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Indoor ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tropicana_Field_Playing_Field_Opening_Day_2010.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Tropicana_Field_Playing_Field_Opening_Day_2010.JPG/250px-Tropicana_Field_Playing_Field_Opening_Day_2010.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Tropicana_Field_Playing_Field_Opening_Day_2010.JPG/375px-Tropicana_Field_Playing_Field_Opening_Day_2010.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Tropicana_Field_Playing_Field_Opening_Day_2010.JPG/500px-Tropicana_Field_Playing_Field_Opening_Day_2010.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tropicana_Field_Playing_Field_Opening_Day_2010.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Tropicana_Field" title="Tropicana Field">Tropicana Field</a>, currently the only active indoor-only MLB baseball park</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An important type of ballpark is the <b>indoor park</b>. These parks were covered with a fixed roof, usually a hard concrete dome. Reasons to build indoor parks were varied. The <a href="/wiki/Astrodome" title="Astrodome">Astrodome</a>, the first indoor sports stadium ever built, was built to escape the hot and very humid climate of Houston; the <a href="/wiki/Kingdome" title="Kingdome">Kingdome</a> was built to escape Seattle's constant rains, and the <a href="/wiki/Sapporo_Dome" title="Sapporo Dome">Sapporo Dome</a> in Japan (which also hosts <a href="/wiki/Consadole_Sapporo" title="Consadole Sapporo">a soccer team</a>) was built to escape Sapporo's extreme snowfall. There is little to no natural light in these parks, necessitating the use of one of the most distinguishing aspects of an indoor park: artificial turf. Since there was not enough light to grow grass, artificial turf was installed, and this affected the game. Artificial turf was harder, and thus a ball hit on the ground moved faster and bounced high. This, coupled with the usually dull white or gray roofs that could camouflage a fly ball, caused what Twins fans called a "dome-field advantage".</p>
<p>A park of note is <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Stadium_(Montreal)" title="Olympic Stadium (Montreal)">Olympic Stadium</a> in Montreal. The park was designed with a large tower that loomed over top. Cables came down from the top of the tower to connect to the large oval center of the roof. This oval center was supposed to be lifted by the cables, opening the park up if the weather was pleasant. However, the mechanism never worked correctly, and what was supposed to be a retractable roof was initially not used, then used for only a short period of time, and later replaced with a permanently fixed roof, making the stadium a strictly indoor facility.</p>
<p>Indoor parks faced many of the same problems of the multi-purpose parks, which was compounded with the added problem of playing an outdoors sport indoors. <a href="/wiki/Tropicana_Field" title="Tropicana Field">Tropicana Field</a> is the only indoor-only or fixed-dome park left hosting a Major League Baseball team, and <a href="/wiki/Rays_Ballpark" title="Rays Ballpark">may be replaced</a> in the near future.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retractable-roof_ballparks">Retractable-roof ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Retractable-roof ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rogers_Centre_open_and_closed.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2600" data-file-width="5584" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Rogers_Centre_open_and_closed.jpg/300px-Rogers_Centre_open_and_closed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Rogers_Centre_open_and_closed.jpg/450px-Rogers_Centre_open_and_closed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Rogers_Centre_open_and_closed.jpg/600px-Rogers_Centre_open_and_closed.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rogers_Centre_open_and_closed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rogers_Centre" title="Rogers Centre">Rogers Centre</a> is the first functional retractable-roof stadium, shown with the roof both opened and closed.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As mentioned above, the indoor parks were built for several different reasons, chief among those weather. However, as multi-purpose parks became unfashionable, so did the drab indoor parks. This led to the creation of <b>retractable-roof parks</b>. These allowed shelter from the elements, but still could be opened on a nice day. To be able to support the roof, most were closed in on all sides like multi-purpose and indoor parks, but as all retractable-roof parks except <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Centre" title="Rogers Centre">Rogers Centre</a> are baseball only, every square foot does not need to be filled with seating, and there is plenty of room for open spaces or windows that look outside. Because the roof needs to go somewhere when not covering the field, a distinguishing characteristic of the retractable roof park is a large extension of the interior spaces to either one side of the field or both sides that the roof sits on when retracted. The only exception to this is <a href="/wiki/Miller_Park_(Milwaukee)" title="Miller Park (Milwaukee)">Miller Park</a> whose fan-shaped roof folds in upon itself and hangs behind the stands down the foul lines. Often, when retracted, the roof still hangs over the field, casting large shadows. This is countered at Miller Park by large panes of glass under the roof. While most seal up when the roof is closed, others are at least partially open, with large gaps that do not let in harsh weather, but don't give the feel of being inside. Except for Rogers Centre, all of these parks feature natural grass.</p>
<p>Pittsburgh's <a href="/wiki/Civic_Arena_(Pittsburgh)" title="Civic Arena (Pittsburgh)">Civic Arena</a> was the first sports building in the world with a retractable roof; however, the building was originally constructed for the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Civic_Light_Opera" title="Pittsburgh Civic Light Opera">Pittsburgh Civic Light Opera</a>, which moved out in 1969 due to dissatisfaction with the acoustics in the arena. The arena's long-term tenants, the NHL's <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Penguins" title="Pittsburgh Penguins">Pittsburgh Penguins</a>, never played with the roof open, and the arena itself was never used for baseball. While Montreal's Olympic Stadium was the first baseball park to have a retractable roof, the roof was plagued by numerous problems, and was never fully used. As a result, it is not generally considered to be a retractable-roof facility. This made Rogers Centre the first fully functional retractable-roof park. It managed to succeed where Olympic Stadium failed, building a multi-section roof that folded upon itself, retracting over the hotel in center field.</p>
<p>Retractable-roof parks can vary greatly in style, from the utilitarian (<a href="/wiki/Rogers_Centre" title="Rogers Centre">Rogers Centre</a>), to those infused with retro elements (such as <a href="/wiki/Minute_Maid_Park" title="Minute Maid Park">Minute Maid Park</a>), to the contemporary (<a href="/wiki/Marlins_Park" title="Marlins Park">Marlins Park</a>). The style of each park reflects the popular architecture of the era in which it was built. (This differs from indoor ballparks — all of which were built during the time of multi-purpose parks, and thus reflected the same "flying-saucer" style.) When <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Centre" title="Rogers Centre">Rogers Centre</a> opened in 1989, baseball was near the end of the modern and multi-purpose era. <a href="/wiki/Chase_Field" title="Chase Field">Chase Field</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safeco_Field" title="Safeco Field">Safeco Field</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minute_Maid_Park" title="Minute Maid Park">Minute Maid Park</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miller_Park_(Milwaukee)" title="Miller Park (Milwaukee)">Miller Park</a> all opened in the middle of the retro era. When <a href="/wiki/Marlins_Park" title="Marlins Park">Marlins Park</a> opened in 2012, it introduced a new and different style, and perhaps the beginning of a new era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a> intend to build their new <a href="/wiki/New_Rangers_Ballpark" title="New Rangers Ballpark">retractible roof ballpark</a> in the retro style similar to their current ballpark, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globe_Life_Park" title="Globe Life Park">Globe Life Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Therefore, the term "retractable-roof ballpark/stadium" is not a description of the overall architectural style of the building, but of the functional aspect of it. For this reason, retractable-roof parks are also dual-listed in style-based types of ballparks. For example, the 4 retractable-roof parks built during the retro era are also considered to be retro-modern ballparks.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retro-classic_ballparks">Retro-classic ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Retro-classic ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(January 2012)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Camden_Yards_2008-3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Camden_Yards_2008-3.jpg/250px-Camden_Yards_2008-3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Camden_Yards_2008-3.jpg/375px-Camden_Yards_2008-3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Camden_Yards_2008-3.jpg/500px-Camden_Yards_2008-3.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Camden_Yards_2008-3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camden_Yards" title="Camden Yards">Camden Yards</a> started the nostalgic craze with a smaller, redbrick and forest-green stadium.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1992, <a href="/wiki/Oriole_Park_at_Camden_Yards" title="Oriole Park at Camden Yards">Oriole Park at Camden Yards</a> opened in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>. Going in a different direction from the multi-purpose and modern ballparks, Camden Yards harkened back to the old jewel box parks. This began the building of a series of parks known as the <b>retro-classic ballparks</b>, or, simply, <b>retro ballparks</b>.</p>
<p>The retro parks were built with all the luxuries of the newer parks, such as luxury boxes and more restrooms and concession areas, along with new additions, such as indoor concourses that are open to the field, allowing fans to always have a view of the game. However (except for a few exceptions harkening back to the wooden ballpark era), the aesthetics shifted back to jewel box conventions, which included the use of green seats, bricks, stone, and green-painted exposed steel.</p>
<p>A major divergence from jewel boxes was the layout of the grandstand. The focus was now on everyone in the park having a good view. Columns were missing as with the modern parks, but the upper deck was drawn back and shrunk, while the middle tiers grew in size, causing a stepped effect. The cantilevered upper deck was no longer a large necessity. However, since these new upper decks were drawn back, the shape of the inclined seating was clearly expressed on the exterior, a feature that is a hallmark of modern parks.</p>
<p>Like the jewel box parks, the outfield fences were angled rather than the gradual curve of the newer parks, and often had quirky dimensions. The requirements for minimum distance to the outfield fences were rarely enforced during this time.</p>
<p>Teams with multi-purpose and indoor parks longed for this beautiful and classic look, and began systematically demolishing them and moving to either retro-classic or retro-modern parks. Since Camden Yards opened, two-thirds of all major league teams have opened new ballparks, each of which contain unique features. The most important feature was that they were built primarily for baseball, although these venues have also hosted football, soccer and ice hockey games. <a href="/wiki/Turner_Field" title="Turner Field">Turner Field</a> was originally constructed as <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Olympic_Stadium" title="Centennial Olympic Stadium">Centennial Olympic Stadium</a> for the <a href="/wiki/1996_Summer_Olympics" title="1996 Summer Olympics">1996 Summer Olympics</a> and was retrofitted to baseball the following year.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Guaranteed_Rate_Field" title="Guaranteed Rate Field">Guaranteed Rate Field</a> has an unusual place in ballpark history. It was the last modern park built, built a year before Camden Yards just missing the retro movement, it was viewed as obsolete only a year into its life. The White Sox responded with a series of renovations to give the park more retro charm. This included the changing from a cantilever upper deck to a flat roof with columns, and the change from a symmetrical fence to a more recognizably-shaped asymmetrical fence.</p>
<p>The most recent retro-classic ballparks were built in New York City. Queens' <a href="/wiki/Citi_Field" title="Citi Field">Citi Field</a> is modeled after <a href="/wiki/Ebbets_Field" title="Ebbets Field">Ebbets Field</a>, and the Bronx's <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium" title="Yankee Stadium">Yankee Stadium</a> is modeled after the pre-renovation "<a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium_(1923)" title="Yankee Stadium (1923)">House that Ruth Built</a>". Both parks opened in 2009.</p>
<p>Teams are now trending away from the retro-classic look and are instead building retro-modern and contemporary ballparks. Turner Field is the first retro-classic park scheduled to be replaced, as the <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Braves" title="Atlanta Braves">Atlanta Braves</a> plan to move to <a href="/wiki/SunTrust_Park" title="SunTrust Park">SunTrust Park</a> after the 2016 season, while the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a> intend to move from <a href="/wiki/Globe_Life_Park_in_Arlington" title="Globe Life Park in Arlington">Globe Life Park in Arlington</a> to a new retractible roof <a href="/wiki/New_Rangers_Ballpark" title="New Rangers Ballpark">ballpark</a> in 2021.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retro-modern_ballparks">Retro-modern ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Retro-modern ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Progressive_Field_sign.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Progressive_Field_sign.jpg/250px-Progressive_Field_sign.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Progressive_Field_sign.jpg/375px-Progressive_Field_sign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Progressive_Field_sign.jpg/500px-Progressive_Field_sign.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Progressive_Field_sign.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Progressive_Field" title="Progressive Field">Progressive Field</a> was the first retro park with a modern exterior</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While Camden Yards influenced nearly every ballpark built after it, not all fully adhere to its design. Those that deviate to incorporate more modern-looking elements are called <b>retro-modern ballparks</b>.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacobs_Field" title="Jacobs Field">Progressive Field</a> was built two years after Camden Yards, and featured the angular, asymmetrical fences of varying heights, a smaller upper deck, stepped tiers, and an unobtrusive singular color scheme. While the interior has all the hallmarks of a retro park, the exterior did not feature the look of the jewel box parks. It could not truly be called a retro-classic park.</p>
<p>Many of today's parks have followed in this second school of retro. Rather than brick, the exteriors heavily feature white- or gray-painted steel. If there is any masonry, it is sandstone or limestone. Some feature progressive elements such as curtain walls, or retractable roofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theatlanticcities.com_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-theatlanticcities.com-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angel_Stadium_of_Anaheim" title="Angel Stadium of Anaheim">Angel Stadium of Anaheim</a> has seen many changes throughout the years. It was originally a modern park, similar to the Angels' previous home, <a href="/wiki/Dodger_Stadium" title="Dodger Stadium">Dodger Stadium</a>. When the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">NFL</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Rams" title="Los Angeles Rams">Los Angeles Rams</a> left the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Memorial_Coliseum" title="Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum">Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum</a> in 1980 and set up shop in what was then Anaheim Stadium, the first round of renovations began. The grandstand was expanded to completely enclose the stadium, turning it into a multi-purpose park. The Rams left in 1994, leaving the Angels alone in the large, 65,000 seat stadium. After a two-year renovation, the steel was painted green, and what concrete remained was painted sandstone, including the sweeping curve of the entrance plaza. The seating configuration was significantly altered, most notably by tearing out most of the outfield seating except for parts of the lower decks in left and right fields, to more closely resemble the original design from the park's first 15 years. The finished product in 1998 was a retro-modern ballpark.</p>
<p>The same year, when <a href="/wiki/Chase_Field" title="Chase Field">Chase Field</a> opened for the expansion <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Diamondbacks" title="Arizona Diamondbacks">Arizona Diamondbacks</a>, it incorporated a retractable roof and a swimming pool — elements that did not exist in jewel-box ballparks. Despite the absence of MLB history in the <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix</a> area and an overwhelming roof design, much of the interior was still built with all of the hallmarks of retro, similar to <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Field" title="Progressive Field">Progressive Field</a>. Although <a href="/wiki/Chase_Field" title="Chase Field">Chase Field</a> was not the <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Centre" title="Rogers Centre">first retractable-roof ballpark</a> in history, it was the first in a wave of four retractable-roof ballparks (opening within just four years) to follow the retro-modern pattern.</p>
<p>During the second decade of retro, <a href="/wiki/Petco_Park" title="Petco Park">Petco Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kauffman_Stadium" title="Kauffman Stadium">Kauffman Stadium</a> followed the construction and renovation concepts of <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Field" title="Progressive Field">Progressive Field</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angel_Stadium_of_Anaheim" title="Angel Stadium of Anaheim">Angel Stadium</a>, respectively. Meanwhile, the period saw another subset of three new retro-modern stadiums that pushed away from classic parks even more.</p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Ball_Park" title="Great American Ball Park">Great American Ball Park</a> opened in 2003, it featured a contemporary-looking, glass-wrapped facade. Such prominent use of elements that were unfamiliar even to pre-1992 modern stadiums signaled that some stadium planners were more willing to incorporate designs that looked into the 21st century as much as they did the 20th. Five years later, <a href="/wiki/Nationals_Park" title="Nationals Park">Nationals Park</a> built off Cincinnati's design, making yet more liberal use of glass along with white concrete that would not clash with architecture in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">the District</a>. Nationals Park became the first stadium to <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">go green</a> while still offering all of the amenities — another concept that looked ahead instead of behind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The retro-modern style climaxed in 2010 with the sculptured, contemporary exterior and canopy of <a href="/wiki/Target_Field" title="Target Field">Target Field</a>, rendering it almost unrecognizable from the outside. Its cantilevered glass on top of a limestone base was designed partly to functionally fit the tiny 8-acre plot in the middle of a bustling transportation interchange. But the principal architect of Target Field, Earl Santee of <a href="/wiki/Populous_(company)" title="Populous (company)">Populous</a>, said that the exterior was also an artistic interpretation of the culture of Minnesotans: a dichotomy of cosmopolitan and natural.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Designing the building as a metaphor for people was a different way of thinking about ballpark architecture.</p>
<p>The exterior of the later retro-contemporary trio of ballparks progressively evolved further and further from jewel-box or even modern-style parks. Yet, in the stands and on the field they still have the familiar classic feel while implementing the marks of retro (i.e., unique-shaped fences, forest green or other singular color scheme, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theatlanticcities.com_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-theatlanticcities.com-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
