<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_ballparks">Contemporary ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Contemporary ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Advert" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>contains content that is written like <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_a_soapbox_or_means_of_promotion" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">an advertisement</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by removing <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Spam" title="Wikipedia:Spam">promotional content</a> and inappropriate <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:External_links" title="Wikipedia:External links">external links</a>, and by adding encyclopedic content written from a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutral point of view</a>.</span> <small><i>(November 2013)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marlins_Park_front_plaza.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Marlins_Park_front_plaza.jpg/250px-Marlins_Park_front_plaza.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Marlins_Park_front_plaza.jpg/375px-Marlins_Park_front_plaza.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Marlins_Park_front_plaza.jpg/500px-Marlins_Park_front_plaza.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marlins_Park_front_plaza.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Marlins_Park" title="Marlins Park">Marlins Park</a> has a contemporary, Miami-centric design with a sculptural glass &amp; curves depiction of "water merging with land", Miami-Deco tiles, and a bright multi-color scheme.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Prior to the start of the 2012 baseball season, <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a></i> noted that new Marlins Park "likely is the last major league ballpark to be built for another five to 10 years", and speculated that it would "perhaps provide a coda to the <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_architecture" title="Postmodern architecture">postmodern</a> Camden Yards era".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_Today_Marlins_Park_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-USA_Today_Marlins_Park-14">[14]</a></sup> After 2 decades of the retro style dominating ballpark architecture, a new type of design emerged in 2012 with the opening of <a href="/wiki/Marlins_Park" title="Marlins Park">Marlins Park</a>, snapping the consecutive streak of 20 new (plus 3 renovated) MLB retro stadiums. This latest style's purpose is to make the fan experience the present-day culture of the stadium's surrounding city or area, and rejects the basic notion of retro. Stadium planners are calling the style <b><a href="/wiki/Contemporary_architecture" title="Contemporary architecture">contemporary</a>.</b></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Rays_Ballpark" title="Rays Ballpark">contemporary stadium for Tampa Bay</a> was designed, but the project was cancelled. <i>The New Yorker</i> wrote regarding the new MLB architecture: "The retro mold has finally been broken, but this might be the last chance a new style gets for some time." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>

</tr>

<td><a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a></td>

<p><img alt="double-dagger" data-file-height="14" data-file-width="9" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f9/Double-dagger-14-plain.png" width="9"/> denotes stadium is also a retractable-roof ballpark</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_Major_League_ballparks">Current Major League ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Current Major League ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_stadiums" title="List of Major League Baseball stadiums">List of Major League Baseball stadiums</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CardinalDimensions.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="293" data-file-width="273" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/af/CardinalDimensions.png/220px-CardinalDimensions.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/af/CardinalDimensions.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CardinalDimensions.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The cardinal outfield dimensions, along with the backstop.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The numbers mean the number of feet from home plate to the wall of that part of the field. Left and Right Field normally refer to the distances along the foul lines. Left Center and Right Center are the approximate power alley figures. Center Field could mean straightaway center field or it could mean to the deepest part of the center field area. Backstop refers to the distance behind home plate to the backstop screen. These numbers <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> are one researcher's opinion of the true values and may differ from the numbers marked on the wall/fence by as much as 30 feet (9.1 m). Capacity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> figures may also vary.</p>

†Actual distance to center field is 400 feet (122 m); the 395 feet (120 m) markings are to the left and right of dead center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cathedrals_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cathedrals-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>††At Fenway Park, deep center is 379 feet (116 m) and straightaway center is 389.75 feet (119 m).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Unique_features_and_quirks_of_current_major_league_parks">Unique features and quirks of current major league parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baseball_park&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Unique features and quirks of current major league parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>Parks are ordered by date of construction.</p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a>) – Fenway is quite possibly the most notoriously quirky ballpark. The aforementioned 37-foot (11 m) <a href="/wiki/Green_Monster" title="Green Monster">Green Monster</a> in left field, which includes a field-level manually operated scoreboard and a ladder, leads to unpredictable ricochets. So do the oddly angled fences in center and right field. While the Monster in left dominates the park, Fenway's right field is also notable. Known as "the belly", right field is 380 at its deepest point, a large divergence from the short left field. The curve of the fence comes in as it nears "Pesky's Pole", named for former Red Sox player <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Pesky" title="Johnny Pesky">Johnny Pesky</a>, and the distance from home plate to the foul pole is only 302 feet (92 m). Strangely enough, despite this being the shortest foul line in baseball, home runs in this area are relatively rare.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wrigley_Field" title="Wrigley Field">Wrigley Field</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a>) – Wrigley is the only ballpark left in use that has a brick outfield fence. This fence is covered in Wrigley's trademark <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ivy" title="Ivy">ivy</a>. From time to time, a batted ball becomes lodged in the vines, which is ruled a <a href="/wiki/Ground_rule_double" title="Ground rule double">ground rule double</a>. Because there are streets close beyond the outfield fences, there is little room for seating or other structures, making the park very open in the outfield. This, coupled with the park's proximity to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Lake Michigan</a>, which affects the wind currents from day to day or even hour to hour, can change the dynamic of the park. When the wind is blowing in, the park becomes a pitcher's park, despite the cozy power alleys. When the wind is blowing out, however, it becomes a hitters park. The openness of the ballpark also allows residents of the buildings across the street to get an unobstructed view of the field. Some owners even have installed small sections of inclined seating on top of the buildings. Wrigley was the last major league park to install field lights, with the first night home game being played in 1988.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Dodger_Stadium" title="Dodger Stadium">Dodger Stadium</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a>) – The view beyond the outfield is dominated by hills and the San Gabriel Mountains, and on one of the hills, large block letters have been erected that spell out "THINK BLUE", reminiscent of the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Sign" title="Hollywood Sign">Hollywood Sign</a> (which can be seen from the top deck of the stadium facing away from the field). The setting sun often reflects off the hills and can give the pitchers a distinct advantage, which the Dodgers have capitalized upon frequently. The Dodger Stadium grandstand is sculpted into the side of a man-made hill, in the fashion of the ancient Roman amphitheaters. This enabled the construction of multileveled terraced parking lots, which allow patrons to park their cars on the same level as their seats, thus minimizing vertical climbing and the need for elevators, escalators and ramps. One perceived drawback of this design is that it does not allow spectators to easily move from one seating level to another. The top of a ten-story elevator shaft bearing the Dodger logo rises behind home plate atop the upper seating level. The massive tiers consist of varicolored seats (yellow, light orange, turquoise, and sky blue), and the right and left field seats consist of little-league-style bleachers topped by a wavy roof. Dodger Stadium has become famous for the no-gimmick, nostalgic baseball atmosphere created by the aesthetic simplicity of the ballpark's design. The slogan for the stadium is "Blue Heaven on Earth".</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angel_Stadium_of_Anaheim" title="Angel Stadium of Anaheim">Angel Stadium of Anaheim</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Angels_of_Anaheim" title="Los Angeles Angels of Anaheim">Los Angeles Angels of Anaheim</a>) – Beyond the left field fence is a rock outcropping with water running down the center of it. At the top is three rocks forming an "A". Commonly known as the California Spectacular (or simply the Spectacular), the outcropping contains fake geyser spouts that erupt from the water after homeruns and wins. The roof over the dugouts continues, connecting the two to form a dugout-level seating. The parking lot contains a large "A" with a halo, which once held the stadium's scoreboard. There are also two giant red caps at the front gate with the cement painted in the form of a miniature baseball diamond in front of the home plate entrance. Behind the caps are oversized bats attached to a baseball which "hold-up" the stadium marquee and is attached to the façade.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oakland_Coliseum" title="Oakland Coliseum">Oakland Coliseum</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Oakland_Athletics" title="Oakland Athletics">Oakland Athletics</a>) – The Coliseum is dominated by a section of seating derisively known as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Davis_(Oakland)" title="Mount Davis (Oakland)">Mount Davis</a>". To lure the <a href="/wiki/Oakland_Raiders" title="Oakland Raiders">Raiders</a> football team from Los Angeles back to Oakland, seats needed to be added to satisfy owner <a href="/wiki/Al_Davis" title="Al Davis">Al Davis</a>. Thus, the 6-floor seating area was added, blocking the view of the hills beyond. The Coliseum is also known for its immense foul territory, which results in many popped-up foul balls that would go well into the seats in other stadiums being easily caught for outs.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Kauffman_Stadium" title="Kauffman Stadium">Kauffman Stadium</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Royals" title="Kansas City Royals">Kansas City Royals</a>) – Kauffman is best known for having a 322-foot (98 m) fountain and waterfall display, known as the Water Spectacular, the largest privately funded fountain in the world. The fountains are on display before and after the game and in-between innings, while the waterfalls are constantly flowing. The 12-story iconic crown-shaped scoreboard holds the largest video board in MLB. The stadium is also noted for having the best <a href="/wiki/Batter%27s_eye" title="Batter's eye">hitter's background</a> in the American League.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Rogers_Centre" title="Rogers Centre">Rogers Centre</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Toronto_Blue_Jays" title="Toronto Blue Jays">Toronto Blue Jays</a>)- The first fully functional retractable roof over a baseball stadium rests here, and is a unique system, with the larger section folding into a smaller semicircle that looms over the field. Under this sits glassed-in seating and one of the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Jumbotron" title="Jumbotron">Jumbotron</a> screens. The 348-room <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Hotels" title="Renaissance Hotels">Renaissance</a> Toronto Hotel is also located within stadium, with 70 rooms overlooking the field. The <a href="/wiki/CN_Tower" title="CN Tower">CN Tower</a> next door stands tall over the park. This stadium is the last of the "squared-circle" designs that were similar to <a href="/wiki/Qualcomm_Stadium" title="Qualcomm Stadium">Qualcomm Stadium</a> and the now-demolished <a href="/wiki/Veterans_Stadium" title="Veterans Stadium">Veterans Stadium</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Guaranteed_Rate_Field" title="Guaranteed Rate Field">Guaranteed Rate Field</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago White Sox</a>) – The old <a href="/wiki/Comiskey_Park" title="Comiskey Park">Comiskey Park</a> was well known for what was called the "Exploding Scoreboard". This scoreboard was reincarnated in the new park. Holding a huge video screen, the scoreboard features sound effects and fireworks that go off after wins and home runs and is topped by iconic spinning pinwheels.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Oriole_Park_at_Camden_Yards" title="Oriole Park at Camden Yards">Oriole Park at Camden Yards</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">Baltimore Orioles</a>) – Camden Yards is the park that began the retro ballpark craze (although the minor-league <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Field" title="Coca-Cola Field">Coca-Cola Field</a> in <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/Populous_(company)" title="Populous (company)">the same firm</a>, predates Camden Yards by four years). Its green seats, its brick and steel finish, and its irregular grandstand configuration all hearken back to the good ol' days. Beyond the bleachers in right and right center is a wall of brick columns with black iron gates. Beyond that looms the large <a href="/wiki/B%26O_Warehouse" title="B&amp;O Warehouse">B&amp;O Warehouse</a>, a very prominent feature of the park.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Progressive_Field" title="Progressive Field">Progressive Field</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Indians" title="Cleveland Indians">Cleveland Indians</a>) – This is the first retro-modern ballpark of the era. Cleveland has a 19-foot (5.8 m) left field wall, known as "The Little Green Monster", complete with a digital scoreboard embedded in the wall, installed in 2015.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Globe_Life_Park_in_Arlington" title="Globe Life Park in Arlington">Globe Life Park in Arlington</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a>) – The exterior is a solid one of brick and features stone <a href="/wiki/Texas_Longhorn" title="Texas Longhorn">longhorn</a> steer heads. The stadium is completely closed in, with offices walling in the space beyond the outfield. This can create a swirling wind effect that gives some hits a bit of aerodynamic lift. Right-center field fence has an inward "jog", a feature of artificial quirkiness that several recent ballparks include.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coors_Field" title="Coors Field">Coors Field</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Colorado_Rockies" title="Colorado Rockies">Colorado Rockies</a>) – Most of the seats in Coors Field are dark green, however, the seats in the 20th row of the upper deck are purple. This marks the city's one mile (5,280 feet) elevation point. While most stadiums' batter's eyes are dark colored walls or patches of grass, Coors Field takes the opportunity to feature a scene of Colorado nature. The area, which sits in front of a tall green wall, is a large rock outcropping with a few ponds and waterfalls, and a number of evergreen trees. Atop this "rockpile" is a standalone and very distant bleacher section. The higher altitude and lower air density of Denver also figure into the uniqueness of the ballpark.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Tropicana_Field" title="Tropicana Field">Tropicana Field</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay_Rays" title="Tampa Bay Rays">Tampa Bay Rays</a>) – Tropicana Field is a field that has had its share of criticisms, the biggest having to do with its catwalks. Four catwalks encircle the dome's interior, with the two towards the infield being in play. A ball hit off of them can be caught for an out. The two towards the outfield are out of play and, when hit, are either a home run or a foul ball, depending on where it hits in relation to the foul poles. Unlike most former MLB facilities with artificial turf, the park has a full dirt infield, rather than limiting dirt to "sliding pits" around each base. Also included is a "Touch Tank" where visitors can pet rays. Various elements from the former <a href="/wiki/Citrus_Bowl" title="Citrus Bowl">Citrus Bowl</a> have also been implemented into a fresco mosaic outside of the venue.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Chase_Field" title="Chase Field">Chase Field</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Arizona_Diamondbacks" title="Arizona Diamondbacks">Arizona Diamondbacks</a>) – Due to the hot Phoenix weather, Chase is fully air conditioned. It also has a pool in the outfield, which can be rented by spectators. The field itself is graced by a distinctive dirt strip between home plate and the pitching mound. Once uniformly common in early baseball parks, this strip is present in only two modern parks (Comerica Park being the other).</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Safeco_Field" title="Safeco Field">Safeco Field</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Seattle_Mariners" title="Seattle Mariners">Seattle Mariners</a>) – Safeco is known for its unique retractable roof: unlike most retractable roofs which completely enclose their stadiums, Safeco's acts more like an umbrella (allowing the surface to be protected from Seattle's frequent rainfall without closing the entire park). The roof is supported by large steel structures that run on tracks, and these structures are very open, barely obscuring the view to the outside.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Park" title="AT&amp;T Park">AT&amp;T Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Giants" title="San Francisco Giants">San Francisco Giants</a>) – An arm of <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Bay" title="San Francisco Bay">San Francisco Bay</a> is just outside the right field fence. The area is called <a href="/wiki/McCovey_Cove" title="McCovey Cove">McCovey Cove</a> (named for former Giants slugger <a href="/wiki/Willie_McCovey" title="Willie McCovey">Willie McCovey</a>), and is often filled with boaters hoping to catch baseballs hit out of the park (a baseball initially floats in water), just as fans used to leave the right field bleachers at <a href="/wiki/Candlestick_Park" title="Candlestick Park">Candlestick Park</a> and gather on the flat ground when McCovey came to bat. The right field line is rather close, and although the wall angles away sharply, a "jog" in right-center neutralizes that angle somewhat. Underneath this 24-foot (7.3 m) high brick wall is an area where fans can watch (a maximum of three innings) the ballgame for free. There is a special scoreboard near the right field wall that counts how many fair balls have been hit into the bay during game play by the San Francisco Giants (called "Splash Hits"). Many of those have been hit by <a href="/wiki/Barry_Bonds" title="Barry Bonds">Barry Bonds</a>, the left-handed slugger whose presence strongly influenced the design of the ballpark, as legend says Yankee Stadium's short porch was for <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a>. The grass field is one of a few ballparks to not have patterns mowed into it creating what the Giants called "an authentic old-time feel" to the ballpark. Beyond the center-field wall and below the batters-eye the groundskeepers have planted an <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocado</a> tree. It is also the only current Major League ballpark with a female public-address announcer (<a href="/wiki/Renel_Brooks-Moon" title="Renel Brooks-Moon">Renel Brooks-Moon</a>).</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Comerica_Park" title="Comerica Park">Comerica Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a>) – Two brick walls flank the batter's eye, the names of those important to the organization and the Tigers' retired numbers painted in white on them. Above the left field wall, Comerica has its own monument park filled with large statues of Tiger greats. Over the left field upper-deck, one can see <a href="/wiki/Ford_Field" title="Ford Field">Ford Field</a> looming, which was built in the same project as the park. The scoreboard is topped by two large tigers, and, whenever a run is scored, their eyes light up and the sound of a tiger growl is played over the speakers. Beyond the outfield is a great view of downtown Detroit, including the Wyland Whale mural, although this has been periodically covered with a Verizon ad since the 2006 postseason. Since downtown is located south of the stadium, this orientation unfortunately gives the majority of spectators in left field an eyeful of sunlight as the sun sets. The park started its life with an extremely deep left-center field, but the fence has since been moved in with the bullpen now occupying the previously in-play area. It is interesting to note that the left-center field flag pole was originally in-play before this fence was brought in prior to the 2003 season. The field itself is graced by a distinctive dirt strip between home plate and the pitching mound. Once uniformly common in early baseball parks, this strip is present in only two modern parks (Chase Field being the other).</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Minute_Maid_Park" title="Minute Maid Park">Minute Maid Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Houston_Astros" title="Houston Astros">Houston Astros</a>) – A train filled with giant oranges runs forward and back along the left outfield when home runs are hit. The train was placed at Minute Maid in honor of the stadium being built in what was once the rail terminal for Houston. The old <a href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Houston)" title="Union Station (Houston)">Union Station</a> stands beyond the train on the wall and serves as the main entrance for the ballpark. Before its removal after the 2016 season, center field featured <a href="/wiki/Minute_Maid_Park#Features" title="Minute Maid Park">an uphill incline known as "Tal's Hill"</a>, much like old <a href="/wiki/Crosley_Field" title="Crosley Field">Crosley Field</a> in Cincinnati and the former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duffy%27s_Cliff" title="Duffy's Cliff">Duffy's Cliff</a> at Fenway Park in Boston, although it was created as a decoration, as opposed to being required due to the grade of the land. Also, before the center field fence was moved in after the 2016 season, it was the only park in major league baseball with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagpole" title="Flagpole">flagpole</a> in play, another purely decorative feature borrowed from the old <a href="/wiki/Tiger_Stadium_(Detroit)" title="Tiger Stadium (Detroit)">Tiger Stadium</a> in Detroit. First baseman <a href="/wiki/Richie_Sexson" title="Richie Sexson">Richie Sexson</a>, then of the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Brewers" title="Milwaukee Brewers">Milwaukee Brewers</a>, is the only player to have hit the flagpole during a game. Its cozy dimensions, especially in left field, have led to its being called the "Juice Box". The Astros have been competitive in the early years of the ballpark, and the stadium fans can be very noisy when the roof is closed.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Miller_Park_(Milwaukee)" title="Miller Park (Milwaukee)">Miller Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Brewers" title="Milwaukee Brewers">Milwaukee Brewers</a>) – Like Safeco Field, Miller Park has a retractable roof. In the left field corner houses "Bernie's Dugout", the home of the Brewers mascot, <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Brewer" title="Bernie Brewer">Bernie Brewer</a>. At the beginning of the game, after every Brewers <a href="/wiki/Home_run" title="Home run">home run</a>, and if the Brewers win, fireworks go off and Bernie slides down his slide. He also hangs "K"s to signify strikeouts. In 2006, a picnic area was added in right field. The retractable roof is unique in that it folds and unfolds like a fan, from a single pivot point, rather than sliding in parallel sections as most do. The arc-shaped trusses needed to support this roof make the ballpark nearly twice as high as it would be without a roof, dominating its surroundings.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/PNC_Park" title="PNC Park">PNC Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Pirates" title="Pittsburgh Pirates">Pittsburgh Pirates</a>) – The park was built on the <a href="/wiki/Allegheny_River" title="Allegheny River">Allegheny River</a>, and its low walls behind the outfield seats allow for a stunning view of the river and skyline of downtown Pittsburgh that rests on the other side. Located directly outside the park is the <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Clemente_Bridge" title="Roberto Clemente Bridge">Roberto Clemente Bridge</a>, named in honor of the Pirates' legend, which is closed to traffic during games and serves as a pedestrian bridge.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Great_American_Ball_Park" title="Great American Ball Park">Great American Ball Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Reds" title="Cincinnati Reds">Cincinnati Reds</a>) – Sitting on the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>, the park has the same relationship to the water that AT&amp;T and PNC have. Beyond the right-center fence sits two large steamboat smokestacks that belch smoke, flash lights, and shoot fireworks when the Reds hit a home run and win games. The most prominent feature is a large gap in the grandstand. As the outfield is towards the river, and away from downtown Cincinnati, there is a large gap in the grandstands that allow a view out to the city from the park and vice versa. To one side of the gap, there are three seating levels, and on the other, there are two. Inside the park, there are Italian-marble mosaics, "toothbrush" light towers and the nostalgic Sun/Moon Deck. The contemporary-looking, glass-wrapped facade around the exterior is the first of the retro era.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Citizens_Bank_Park" title="Citizens Bank Park">Citizens Bank Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Phillies" title="Philadelphia Phillies">Philadelphia Phillies</a>) – Citizens Bank Park has a giant Liberty Bell that lights up and rings as it moves from side to side. Outfield fence has a "jog" similar to Globe Life Park in Arlington, except it's in left-center. The Phillies' spring training home, Bright House Field in Clearwater, Florida, has the same outfield dimensions as Citizens Bank Park and replicates the "jog" in left-center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Petco_Park" title="Petco Park">Petco Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Padres" title="San Diego Padres">San Diego Padres</a>) – A challenging factor in the design of this stadium was the presence of a historic structure, the Western Metal Supply Company Building, on the proposed ballpark site. Instead of being demolished, the building was integrated into the stadium, with the team store on the first floor and the other floors converted to suites. In fact, its southeast corner serves as the left field foul pole.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Busch_Stadium" title="Busch Stadium">Busch Stadium</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Cardinals</a>) – The stadium is very open, allowing a great view of St. Louis, including the iconic <a href="/wiki/Gateway_Arch" title="Gateway Arch">Gateway Arch</a>. The old manual scoreboards that showed the scores around the league, were installed up on the walls of the inside concourse, still in the same configuration they were in the day the Cardinals played their last game in the old stadium. The right field foul pole from Busch II was never moved and became the left field foulpole when the stadiums position was rotated to its current resting spot.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Nationals_Park" title="Nationals Park">Nationals Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Washington_Nationals" title="Washington Nationals">Washington Nationals</a>) – The park, located on the <a href="/wiki/Anacostia_River" title="Anacostia River">Anacostia River</a>, provides views of the river as well as the dome of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">Capitol Building</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument" title="Washington Monument">Washington Monument</a>. The seats in center field are lined along the rear with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cherry_tree" title="Cherry tree">cherry trees</a>, like those that dominate the landscape of the <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">Potomac</a> shoreline a few miles away – the initial blooming of the trees, a <a href="/wiki/National_Cherry_Blossom_Festival" title="National Cherry Blossom Festival">significant event</a> for the city, usually coincides with the opening of the baseball season. Extensive glass with white steel and pre-cast concrete reflect the architecture of the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">District of Columbia</a>. It is the first stadium to receive <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">LEED</a> certification as a <a href="/wiki/Green_building" title="Green building">green building</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Citi_Field" title="Citi Field">Citi Field</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/New_York_Mets" title="New York Mets">New York Mets</a>) – Citi Field is the only ballpark in the majors that does not have yellow foul poles, as its poles are instead painted orange, a trait it shares with its predecessor, <a href="/wiki/Shea_Stadium" title="Shea Stadium">Shea Stadium</a>. The exterior facade is reminiscent of <a href="/wiki/Ebbets_Field" title="Ebbets Field">Ebbets Field</a>, and leads into the <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Robinson" title="Jackie Robinson">Jackie Robinson</a> Rotunda, which honors the Brooklyn Dodgers' legend. The most famous quirk of Citi Field, a Mets tradition that has been present since 1982, is the home-run apple. Now in straightaway center in the middle of the batter's eye, there is a concrete housing. When the Mets hit a home run, the giant apple, which has a Mets logo on the front that lights up, rises from its housing.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium" title="Yankee Stadium">Yankee Stadium</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a>) – The batter's eye in center field is a sports bar with tinted windows. Under this bar is <a href="/wiki/Monument_Park_(Yankee_Stadium)" title="Monument Park (Yankee Stadium)">Monument Park</a>, an outdoor museum dedicated to the history of the team, which includes plaques, the retired numbers, and the six eponymous monuments, plaques placed on slabs of marble. The exterior is designed to resemble the old Yankee Stadium, but is actually a shell, with an open "Great Hall" between the exterior and the structure of the actual stadium. An iconic feature of the old stadium is the arched frieze, which is now placed back to its original position as the <a href="/wiki/Fascia_(architecture)" title="Fascia (architecture)">fascia</a> of the roof.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Target_Field" title="Target Field">Target Field</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Twins" title="Minnesota Twins">Minnesota Twins</a>) – The stadium itself is sculpture design with a local limestone foundation under cantilevered glass. This contemporary exterior is meant as a metaphor for the cultural dichotomy of Minnesotans who are both natural and cosmopolitan. A canopy lines the top edges over the upper deck. The ballpark has heated viewing areas and a heated field. In center field, a modernized version of the original "Minnie and Paul Shaking Hands" logo has mechanical features when a Twins player hits a home run. The sign lights up with strobe lights surrounding the Minnesota state outline and Minne and Paul.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Marlins_Park" title="Marlins Park">Marlins Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Miami_Marlins" title="Miami Marlins">Miami Marlins</a>) – The contemporary-style ballpark is the first non-retro stadium built in the 20 years since Camden Yards opened, breaking a streak of 20 consecutive new (plus 3 renovated) retro ballparks. Marlins Park is designed to be all about 21st-century Miami with its people, <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> and climate. The building is an air-conditioned, retractable-roof ballpark with separate, retractable glass walls beyond the fences. This engineering provides fans with uninterrupted views of downtown Miami while comforting them from Miami's intense heat, humidity, and frequent rain during the Summer. It is also designed to withstand strong hurricanes. The glass exterior meets with sculpted, upward curving white stucco and steel. This reflects contemporary Miami architecture but is also an artistic depiction of the water-meets-land topography of the city. Four bright colors taken from the palette of Spanish artist <a href="/wiki/Joan_Mir%C3%B3" title="Joan Miró">Joan Miró</a> mark zones around park, and the fences are a flashy lime green. Beyond left field, Marlins Park features a South-Beach-themed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Night_club" title="Night club">night club</a> with a bar, loud music, and <a href="/wiki/Swimming_pool" title="Swimming pool">swimming pool</a>. A 75-foot home run sculpture behind center field lights up and animates when the home team hits one out. Dual tropical <a href="/wiki/Aquarium" title="Aquarium">aquariums</a> are housed within either side of the backstop with special glass to protect from impacts. There's a Bobblehead Museum, and plenty of local cuisine, including Cuban tastes of the park's <a href="/wiki/Little_Havana" title="Little Havana">Little Havana</a> neighborhood. The park <a href="/wiki/Green_building" title="Green building">goes green</a> in a several ways: extensive glass to allow in natural light (even when the roof and walls are closed), bamboo paneling in suites instead of hardwood, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urinals#Waterless_urinals" title="Urinals">waterless urinals</a>. Located on the site of the former <a href="/wiki/Miami_Orange_Bowl" title="Miami Orange Bowl">Orange Bowl</a>, the letters from the stadium's predecessor have been scattered around the exterior of the stadium.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/SunTrust_Park" title="SunTrust Park">SunTrust Park</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Braves" title="Atlanta Braves">Atlanta Braves</a>) – Set to open in 2017, this retro-modern design has cantilevered middle and upper decks to place fans closer to the action, plus a canopy covering about 60% of the seats. The park will be oriented with the batter facing southwest (an uncommon feature), giving fans seated behind home plate views of the Atlanta skyline. While the outfield distances are only slightly asymmetrical, the outfield walls vary dramatically in height throughout the stadium.</li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-mlblogs.com-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-mlblogs.com_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-mlblogs.com_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://tribevibe.mlblogs.com/2014/08/29/baseball-heritage-museum-moves-to-fitting-place-renovated-league-park/" rel="nofollow">"Baseball Heritage Museum moves to fitting place — renovated League Park « TribeVibe"</a>. <i>TribeVibe</i><span class="reference-accessdate">.
